## Transcript

- [00:00] In your lifetime, you have probably stared down any number of ability tests and course
- [00:04] exams and PSATs, SATs, ACTs, GREs, GCSEs, whatever you got in your country.
- [00:09] Humans, it seems, really get a thrill out of measuring, ranking, and comparing each
- [00:13] other's intelligence.
- [00:15] Unfortunately, as you saw last week, historically, we have been kind of bad at that.
- [00:18] Today, we think of intelligence as determined by a series of factors related to genetics,
- [00:24] environment, education, perhaps even randomness itself, some aspects of which may correlate
- [00:28] with belonging to a particular social group, and others not.
- [00:31] The key here, though, is that we don't fully understand how or how much some of these factors
- [00:36] work.
- [00:37] How do elements like personal history and conditions like poverty, access to education,
- [00:40] stress, even nutrition affect someone's scores on cognitive tests?
- [00:44] And if a group of people share some of these conditions, how will they respond--both as
- [00:48] individuals and as a group--to certain potentially biased intelligence tests?
- [00:52] In the end, the irony is that in our ongoing effort to measure human intelligence, most
- [00:57] of what we've learned is simply what we don't know.
- [01:05] [Intro]
- [01:10] What is a piano?
- [01:12] Which one of these things is least like the others?
- [01:15] Juice is to glass as hand is to what?
- [01:17] Which one of these numbers does not belong in the series?
- [01:19] Bernice had x number of jelly beans.
- [01:20] She ate one, then gave half of what was left to Bruno, then she ate another and gave half
- [01:25] of the remainder to her dog.
- [01:26] Now, she only has five beans.
- [01:27] How many did she--ahheh uhhhh.
- [01:29] These questions are similar to what you'd find on today's most widely-used intelligence
- [01:33] tests.
- [01:34] The Wechsler Adult Intelligence Scale, or WAIS, and the Wechsler Intelligence Scale
- [01:38] for Children, or WISC.
- [01:40] Originally published by psychologist David Wechsler in 1955, the current edition of the
- [01:44] exam consists of fifteen different sub-tests that assess things like vocabulary, similarities
- [01:49] between objects and concepts, and patterns in letters and numbers.
- [01:54] Cognitive tests usually fall into one of two categories: achievement, or the kind that
- [01:58] reflect what you've learned, and aptitude - the kind that's supposed to predict your
- [02:02] ability to learn something new.
- [02:04] So the WAIS and the WISC are aptitude tests, and your final exam at the end of your math
- [02:07] class is an achievement test.
- [02:09] So, how do we know if an intelligence test, or any other test for that matter, is actually
- [02:13] any good?
- [02:14] Well, today, we do have some standards.
- [02:16] To be widely accepted, a test must hit three important marks.
- [02:19] It has to be standardized, reliable, and valid.
- [02:23] Standardization is basically about comparability.
- [02:25] Whether you answer 15 or 50 questions correctly on a test actually means very little until
- [02:30] you can compare those scores against how others performed.
- [02:34] So, to achieve meaningful comparisons, test-makers must first give the test to a representative
- [02:39] sample group, which sets a standard by which to compare future test-takers.
- [02:42] You've probably heard of a bell-curve.
- [02:44] Whether you're measuring height or mental aptitude or love of the Beatles, it's often
- [02:48] assumed that everyone you're measuring will fall into what's called a normal pattern:
- [02:53] most scores will fall in the mid-range, while fewer hit the extremes.
- [02:57] And actually, it's those extremes that intelligence tests are most widely and effectively used
- [03:01] for.
- [03:02] It might help an educator identify a gifted student who totally blows the roof off a test,
- [03:06] but they're also useful in helping clinicians determine who might have a disability or be
- [03:10] facing some specific barrier.
- [03:11] With victims of traumatic brain injury or stroke, for example, a WAIS test can do a
- [03:16] nice job of sussing out whether a patient who's struggling with language actually has
- [03:19] a problem remembering and identifying words, or if they're just having a hard time processing
- [03:24] the information quickly.
- [03:25] But these tests should be regarded more skeptically when it comes to issues that are either way
- [03:29] more specific or way more broad.
- [03:31] Like, they won't be able to answer questions along the lines of, "Will Jesse get into Harvard?"
- [03:36] or "Are women smarter than men?"
- [03:38] They're just not designed to do that.
- [03:40] And in any case, simply knowing where you fall on a normal curve on a standardized test
- [03:43] doesn't mean much if the test is poorly designed, so along with being standardized, a good test
- [03:48] has high reliability, meaning it yields dependably consistent results.
- [03:53] One way to determine this is to have people take the same test a second time, or some
- [03:56] similar version of it.
- [03:57] If the two performances resemble one another -- if the scores correlate -- then the test
- [04:01] is thought to have good reliability.
- [04:03] And the third requirement for the testing trifecta is simply validity, or the extent
- [04:07] to which a test measures or predicts what it's supposed to.
- [04:10] And there are different kinds of validity -- for instance, if I take the WAIS IQ test
- [04:14] and my scores accurately predict how good my grades in college will be, that's a simple
- [04:19] kind of predictive or criterion validity.
- [04:21] On the other hand, if I take the test and my scores correlate strongly with my results
- [04:25] on another similar cognitive test, like the Stanford Binet, that falls under the broad
- [04:30] category of construct validity.
- [04:32] The key is that all of these are ways to see if a test measures what it claims to measure.
- [04:36] But the stickiest wicket of them all is what we make of the test scores themselves.
- [04:40] We've all heard plenty about the influence of both nature and nurture in psychology,
- [04:44] so the big question is: do our genetics influence our intelligence, or does our environment?
- [04:49] And this is an easy question to answer because they both do.
- [04:52] And that is important.
- [04:54] If the history of intelligence testing has taught us anything, it's that assuming everyone
- [04:57] is smart in the same way and for the same reason can lead to disastrously bad conclusions.
- [05:01] So let's look at the scientific evidence, and the best place for that is the wealth
- [05:05] of twin and adoption studies, which have been fascinatingly helpful in illustrating how
- [05:10] genetics and environment can both influence intelligence.
- [05:13] For example, research has shown that identical twins who were raised together have the highest
- [05:17] rate of similarity in intelligence scores of any group.
- [05:21] Fraternal twins who share only half the genes tend to be much less similar in their scores,
- [05:25] even when raised in the same home.
- [05:27] Likewise, neuroimaging studies show that certain brain regions, like those associated with
- [05:31] language, are structurally similar between identical twins, and show similar activity
- [05:36] while doing the same kinds of mental tasks.
- [05:38] The brains of fraternal twins raised in the same home are very similar in some areas,
- [05:42] but less so in others.
- [05:43] But identical twins who are raised together have the same brain, at least, according to
- [05:48] neuroimaging scans.
- [05:49] Some studies even showed that identical twins raised apart from each other show higher intelligence
- [05:53] correlation than fraternal twins raised together.
- [05:56] And maybe even more interesting is that these intelligence correlations actually increase
- [06:01] over time.
- [06:02] In one mega-study of eleven thousand twin pairs in four countries, that correlation
- [06:06] kept increasing from middle-childhood to adolescence to young adulthood and continued to increase
- [06:11] through adulthood.
- [06:12] Similar research has looked at adopted children and compared their scores with those of their
- [06:16] adopted siblings, parents, and biological parents.
- [06:19] And the results can be kind of surprising, because as adopted kids grow up, their mental
- [06:23] similarities to their adoptive families actually get smaller over time until there's virtually
- [06:28] no correlation by adulthood.
- [06:31] Instead, they become more similar in terms of mental aptitude to their biological parents
- [06:35] over time, even if they never met.
- [06:37] In other words: genes appear to matter.
- [06:39] You could take a hundred kids and raise them in the exact same way, and as adults, they'd
- [06:43] still have different aptitudes.
- [06:45] But, does this mean that when it comes to intelligence, we're all nature and no nurture?
- [06:49] Well, luckily, and somewhat obviously, no.
- [06:52] Life experiences and environment also matter.
- [06:55] One sad example of how early environments affect children can be found in the work of
- [06:59] American psychologist J. McVicker Hunt in a destitute Iranian orphanage in the 1970s.
- [07:04] Conditions were really, really bad; infants received minimal care, and whatever attention
- [07:09] they did get was on a routine schedule, never in response to whether they were cooing or
- [07:13] crying or anything else.
- [07:14] Basically, they were being raised with no cause and effect between their behaviors and
- [07:18] the responses of their caregivers, and as a result, they didn't learn how to communicate.
- [07:23] With no stimulation or social response, the kids were just kind of passive, vacant lumps.
- [07:28] Deprivation was essentially trumping any inborn intelligence.
- [07:31] So, Hunt started a program.
- [07:33] He trained caregivers to actually talk to infants, to teach them how to mimic sounds
- [07:37] and actions, and eventually, sounds and words from their language.
- [07:40] The results were tremendous.
- [07:42] The kids started to learn really quickly, and basically just came alive.
- [07:45] While it's an extreme example, Hunt's research showed how malleable early childhood intelligence
- [07:50] can be, especially in disadvantaged and stressful conditions.
- [07:53] So you can see that environment and heredity interact to affect intelligence, and that
- [07:57] some tricky implications can come out of that conclusion.
- [08:00] But that's hardly the only controversy when it comes to how we view and measure intelligence.
- [08:05] There have been some sensational headline-courting studies of genetic and social influences that
- [08:10] have suggested that fundamental differences in intelligence may exist between genders
- [08:15] and races, but many of these studies are tangled up in questions of how potential testing bias
- [08:20] may affect performance.
- [08:21] Basically, if a test inadvertently measures differences caused by cultural experiences
- [08:25] or social factors instead of what we might call "innate intelligence", then you might
- [08:30] say that the test is biased.
- [08:31] Extreme example: in the past, immigrants to the US were classified as "feeble minded"
- [08:36] if they couldn't answer distinctly American questions, like "Who was the first American
- [08:40] president?" or "What's a milkshake?".
- [08:43] Today, concerns about bias focuses on differences among members of the same general culture.
- [08:46] Say, a poor, rural kid who might be plenty smart, but will test low if questions involve
- [08:51] urban, upper-class concepts like taking taxis and drinking tea out of china cups or the
- [08:56] rules of tennis.
- [08:57] So the questions themselves can skew performance results, but who administers the test can
- [09:01] also affect outcomes.
- [09:03] Women tend to do better with a fellow female administrator, and African Americans often
- [09:07] score higher if their test is given by an African American instructor.
- [09:10] And the risk of bias may even fall to the test-takers' own expectations.
- [09:14] For example, many studies have found that if you give a math test to equally capable
- [09:19] men and women, but just before starting, you tell the subjects that women usually score
- [09:23] lower than men, you actually negatively affect the women's performance.
- [09:27] This self-fulfilling concern that you might mess up and inadvertently fulfill some negative
- [09:31] stereotype is called stereotype threat.
- [09:34] It was first described by social psychologist Claude Steele and Joshua Aronson, and it's
- [09:37] been demonstrated frequently across a whole host of interesting studies.
- [09:41] Now, we've only scratched the surface of this mess that is intelligence testing.
- [09:45] An important thing to remember next time you ace or bomb a test is that you are far more
- [09:49] complicated and nuanced than any test score.
- [09:52] Don't let a number puff you up or drag you down, and don't let it define you.
- [09:56] We all have room for self-improvement.
- [09:58] We are all full of infinite surprising potential.
- [10:02] Ah, and answers to the questions I asked earlier: A piano is a musical instrument played using
- [10:06] a keyboard, banana is the least similar to the others, juice is to class as hand is to
- [10:10] glove, the number two does not belong in the series, and Bernice began with twenty-three
- [10:15] jellybeans.
- [10:16] Today, your intelligent mind learned how we currently use WAIS and WISC tests to measure
- [10:21] intelligence, and how important it is that a test be standardized, reliable, and valid.
- [10:26] We also looked at how genetics, environment, testing bias, and stereotype threat can affect
- [10:30] IQ test performance.
